Problem: Consider a root system $(V,R)$ and its dual root system $(V^{\vee},R^{\vee})$. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Problem: Consider a root system $(V,R)$ and its dual root system $(V^{\vee},R^{\vee})$. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Problem: Consider a root system $(V,R)$ and its dual root system $(V^{\vee},R^{\vee})$. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Problem: Consider a root system $(V,R)$ and its dual root system $(V^{\vee},R^{\vee})$. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. Problem: Consider a root system $(V,R)$ and its dual root system $(V^{\vee},R^{\vee})$. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Problem: Consider a root system $(V,R)$ and its dual root system $(V^{\vee},R^{\vee})$. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Problem: Consider a root system $(V,R)$ and its dual root system $(V^{\vee},R^{\vee})$. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Problem: Consider a root system $(V,R)$ and its dual root system $(V^{\vee},R^{\vee})$. A chamber of $R$ is defined as a connected component of the complement in $V$ of the hyperplanes $H_{\alpha} = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha^{\vee} \rangle =0\}$. For a chamber $C$, the corresponding base $B = B(C)$ is the unique vector space basis $\alpha_1, \ldots, \alpha_l$ of roots such that $$C = \{ x \in V : \langle x, \alpha_i^{\vee} \rangle > 0 \text{ for } 1 \leq i \leq l \}.$$ The elements of $B$ are called simple roots. Determine if the set $B^{\vee}=\{\alpha_1^{\vee}, \ldots, \alpha_l^{\vee}\}$, known as the "simple coroots," is always a base of the dual root system $R^{\vee}$. Provide a justification for your answer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

For the dual root system R∨, the simple roots δj must be the unique minimal set such that C is the intersection of ⟨x, δj∨⟩ > 0. But δj∨ is in R, since δj is in R∨. So δj∨ = αj. Therefore, the condition ⟨x, δj∨⟩ > 0 is equivalent to ⟨x, αj⟩ > 0. Therefore, for the dual root system R∨, the chamber C is the intersection of ⟨x, αj⟩ > 0. But in the original system, C is the intersection of ⟨x, αj∨⟩ > 0. Therefore, unless ⟨x, αj⟩ > 0 and ⟨x, αj∨⟩ > 0 define the same chamber, which is only the case if αj and αj∨ are scalar multiples. But αj∨ = 2αj/(αj, αj). Therefore, αj∨ is a scalar multiple of αj. Hence, the half-space ⟨x, αj∨⟩ > 0 is the same as ⟨x, αj⟩ > 0 if and only if the scalar is positive, which it is. Since 2/(αj, αj) is positive, then yes, ⟨x, αj∨⟩ > 0 is equivalent to ⟨x, αj⟩ > 0. Therefore, the chamber C is defined equivalently by either ⟨x, αi∨⟩ > 0 or ⟨x, αi⟩ > 0. Therefore, this shows that the set B∨ is indeed the base for the dual root system R∨ corresponding to the chamber C. Thus, in general, the simple coroots B∨ form a base for the dual root system R∨. But let me verify once again. Suppose that in the dual root system R∨, the simple roots are the simple coroots of R. Then the positive coroots in R∨ would be the non-negative linear combinations of the simple coroots. For β: β∨ = 2β. For α + β: (α + β)∨ = 2(α + β)/(α + β, α + β). Let's compute (α + β, α + β) = (α, α) + 2(α, β) + (β, β). In B2, (α, α) = 2, (β, β) = 1, and (α, β) = -1. Therefore, (α + β, α + β) = 2 + 2*(-1) + 1 = 2 - 2 + 1 = 1. Therefore, (α + β)∨ = 2(α + β)/1 = 2α + 2β. Similarly, for α + 2β: (α + 2β, α + 2β) = (α, α) + 4(α, β) + 4(β, β) = 2 + 4*(-1) + 4*1 = 2 -4 +4 = 2. Therefore, (α + 2β)∨ = 2(α + 2β)/2 = α + 2β. Therefore, the positive coroots in R∨ are α, 2β, 2α + 2β, and α + 2β. Now, the simple coroots B∨ = {α∨, β∨} = {α, 2β}. The positive coroots in R∨ should be the non-negative combinations of α and 2β. This is because the coroot of a coroot \( (\alpha^\vee)^\vee \) is the original root \( \alpha \). Therefore, the chambers, which are connected components of the complement of these hyperplanes, are the same for both \( R \) and \( R^\vee \). 2. **Base Definition**: A base for a root system is a set of simple roots such that every root can be written as a linear combination of these simple roots with all coefficients integers of the same sign. For \( B^\vee \) to be a base for \( R^\vee \), every coroot \( \beta^\vee \in R^\vee \) must be expressible as a non-negative or non-positive integer combination of the simple coroots \( \alpha_i^\vee \). 3. **Duality Relationship**: The dual root system \( R^\vee \) consists of coroots \( \alpha^\vee \) for each root \( \alpha \in R \). The simple coroots \( \alpha_i^\vee \) are scaled versions of the simple roots \( \alpha_i \). For the dual root system \( R^\vee \), the same chamber \( C \) is defined by the intersection of positive half-spaces \( \langle x, \alpha_i \rangle > 0 \), which is equivalent due to the scaling relationship between roots and coroots. 4. **Verification with Examples**: Checking examples such as \( A_1 \), \( A_2 \), \( B_2 \), and \( G_2 \) confirms that the simple coroots form a base for the dual root system. In each case, the simple coroots correspond to the simple roots of the dual system, ensuring that all positive coroots in \( R^\vee \) are non-negative combinations of the simple coroots. Problem: Let $ABC$ be a triangle. There exists a positive real number $k$, such that if the altitudes of triangle $ABC$ are extended past $A$, $B$, and $C$, to $A'$, $B'$, and $C'$, as shown, such that $AA' = kBC$, $BB' = kAC$, and $CC' = kAB$, then triangle $A'B'C'$ is equilateral. [asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair[] A, B, C; pair D, E, F; A[0] = (2,4); B[0] = (0,1); C[0] = (5,0); D = (A[0] + reflect(B[0],C[0])*(A[0]))/2; E = (B[0] + reflect(C[0],A[0])*(B[0]))/2; F = (C[0] + reflect(A[0],B[0])*(C[0]))/2; A[1] = A[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(C[0] - B[0])); B[1] = B[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(A[0] - C[0])); C[1] = C[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(B[0] - A[0])); draw(A[0]--B[0]--C[0]--cycle); draw(A[1]--D); draw(B[1]--E); draw(C[1]--F); label("$A$", A[0], NW); dot("$A'$", A[1], N); label("$B$", B[0], S); dot("$B'$", B[1], SW); label("$C$", C[0], S); dot("$C'$", C[1], SE); [/asy] Find $k$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{2\sqrt{3}}

Reference Answer: We place the diagram in the complex plane, so that the vertices $A$, $A'$, $B$, $B'$, $C$, and $C'$ go to the complex numbers $a$, $a'$, $b$, $b'$, $c$, and $c'$, respectively. To get to $a'$, we rotate the line segment joining $b$ to $c$ by $90^\circ$ (which we achieve by multiplying $c - b$ by $i$). Also, we want $AA' = kBC$, so we multiply this complex number by $k$ as well. Hence, \[a' = a + ki(c - b).\]Similarly, \begin{align*} b' &= b + ki(a - c), \\ c' &= c + ki(b - a). \end{align*}[asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair[] A, B, C; pair D, E, F; A[0] = (2,4); B[0] = (0,1); C[0] = (5,0); D = (A[0] + reflect(B[0],C[0])*(A[0]))/2; E = (B[0] + reflect(C[0],A[0])*(B[0]))/2; F = (C[0] + reflect(A[0],B[0])*(C[0]))/2; A[1] = A[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(C[0] - B[0])); B[1] = B[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(A[0] - C[0])); C[1] = C[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(B[0] - A[0])); draw(A[0]--B[0]--C[0]--cycle); draw(A[1]--D); draw(B[1]--E); draw(C[1]--F); draw(B[1]--A[1]--C[1],dashed); label("$a$", A[0], NW); dot("$a'$", A[1], N); label("$b$", B[0], S); dot("$b'$", B[1], SW); label("$c$", C[0], S); dot("$c'$", C[1], SE); [/asy] We want triangle $A'B'C'$ to be equilateral, so we want $a'$, $b'$, and $c'$ to satisfy \[c' - a' = e^{\pi i/3} (b' - a').\]Substituting our expressions for $a'$, $b'$, and $c'$, and using the fact that \[e^{\pi i/3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i,\]we get \[c + ki(b - a) - a - ki(c - b) = \left( \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i \right) [b + ki(a - c) - a - ki(c - b)].\]Expanding and simplifying both sides, we get \begin{align*} &(-1 - ki) a + 2ki b + (1 - ki) c \\ &= \frac{-k \sqrt{3} - 1 + ki - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot a + \frac{- k \sqrt{3} + 1 + ki + i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot b + (k \sqrt{3} - ki) c. \end{align*}We want the coefficients of $a$, $b$, and $c$ to be equal on both sides. Equating the coefficients of $c$, we get \[1 - ki = k \sqrt{3} - ki,\]so $k = 1/\sqrt{3}$. For this value of $k$, both coefficients of $a$ become $-1 - i/\sqrt{3}$, and both coefficients of $b$ become $2i/\sqrt{3}$. Hence, the value of $k$ that works is $k = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}$.

Hence, \[a' = a + ki(c - b).\]Similarly, \begin{align*} b' &= b + ki(a - c), \\ c' &= c + ki(b - a). \end{align*}[asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair[] A, B, C; pair D, E, F; A[0] = (2,4); B[0] = (0,1); C[0] = (5,0); D = (A[0] + reflect(B[0],C[0])*(A[0]))/2; E = (B[0] + reflect(C[0],A[0])*(B[0]))/2; F = (C[0] + reflect(A[0],B[0])*(C[0]))/2; A[1] = A[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(C[0] - B[0])); B[1] = B[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(A[0] - C[0])); C[1] = C[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(B[0] - A[0])); draw(A[0]--B[0]--C[0]--cycle); draw(A[1]--D); draw(B[1]--E); draw(C[1]--F); draw(B[1]--A[1]--C[1],dashed); label("$a$", A[0], NW); dot("$a'$", A[1], N); label("$b$", B[0], S); dot("$b'$", B[1], SW); label("$c$", C[0], S); dot("$c'$", C[1], SE); [/asy] We want triangle $A'B'C'$ to be equilateral, so we want $a'$, $b'$, and $c'$ to satisfy \[c' - a' = e^{\pi i/3} (b' - a').\]Substituting our expressions for $a'$, $b'$, and $c'$, and using the fact that \[e^{\pi i/3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i,\]we get \[c + ki(b - a) - a - ki(c - b) = \left( \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i \right) [b + ki(a - c) - a - ki(c - b)].\]Expanding and simplifying both sides, we get \begin{align*} &(-1 - ki) a + 2ki b + (1 - ki) c \\ &= \frac{-k \sqrt{3} - 1 + ki - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot a + \frac{- k \sqrt{3} + 1 + ki + i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot b + (k \sqrt{3} - ki) c. \end{align*}We want the coefficients of $a$, $b$, and $c$ to be equal on both sides. Equating the coefficients of $c$, we get \[1 - ki = k \sqrt{3} - ki,\]so $k = 1/\sqrt{3}$. For this value of $k$, both coefficients of $a$ become $-1 - i/\sqrt{3}$, and both coefficients of $b$ become $2i/\sqrt{3}$. Hence, the value of $k$ that works is $k = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}$.

[asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair[] A, B, C; pair D, E, F; A[0] = (2,4); B[0] = (0,1); C[0] = (5,0); D = (A[0] + reflect(B[0],C[0])*(A[0]))/2; E = (B[0] + reflect(C[0],A[0])*(B[0]))/2; F = (C[0] + reflect(A[0],B[0])*(C[0]))/2; A[1] = A[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(C[0] - B[0])); B[1] = B[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(A[0] - C[0])); C[1] = C[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(B[0] - A[0])); draw(A[0]--B[0]--C[0]--cycle); draw(A[1]--D); draw(B[1]--E); draw(C[1]--F); label("$A$", A[0], NW); dot("$A'$", A[1], N); label("$B$", B[0], S); dot("$B'$", B[1], SW); label("$C$", C[0], S); dot("$C'$", C[1], SE); [/asy] Find $k$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: We place the diagram in the complex plane, so that the vertices $A$, $A'$, $B$, $B'$, $C$, and $C'$ go to the complex numbers $a$, $a'$, $b$, $b'$, $c$, and $c'$, respectively. To get to $a'$, we rotate the line segment joining $b$ to $c$ by $90^\circ$ (which we achieve by multiplying $c - b$ by $i$). Also, we want $AA' = kBC$, so we multiply this complex number by $k$ as well. Hence, \[a' = a + ki(c - b).\]Similarly, \begin{align*} b' &= b + ki(a - c), \\ c' &= c + ki(b - a). \end{align*}[asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair[] A, B, C; pair D, E, F; A[0] = (2,4); B[0] = (0,1); C[0] = (5,0); D = (A[0] + reflect(B[0],C[0])*(A[0]))/2; E = (B[0] + reflect(C[0],A[0])*(B[0]))/2; F = (C[0] + reflect(A[0],B[0])*(C[0]))/2; A[1] = A[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(C[0] - B[0])); B[1] = B[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(A[0] - C[0])); C[1] = C[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(B[0] - A[0])); draw(A[0]--B[0]--C[0]--cycle); draw(A[1]--D); draw(B[1]--E); draw(C[1]--F); draw(B[1]--A[1]--C[1],dashed); label("$a$", A[0], NW); dot("$a'$", A[1], N); label("$b$", B[0], S); dot("$b'$", B[1], SW); label("$c$", C[0], S); dot("$c'$", C[1], SE); [/asy] We want triangle $A'B'C'$ to be equilateral, so we want $a'$, $b'$, and $c'$ to satisfy \[c' - a' = e^{\pi i/3} (b' - a').\]Substituting our expressions for $a'$, $b'$, and $c'$, and using the fact that \[e^{\pi i/3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i,\]we get \[c + ki(b - a) - a - ki(c - b) = \left( \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i \right) [b + ki(a - c) - a - ki(c - b)].\]Expanding and simplifying both sides, we get \begin{align*} &(-1 - ki) a + 2ki b + (1 - ki) c \\ &= \frac{-k \sqrt{3} - 1 + ki - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot a + \frac{- k \sqrt{3} + 1 + ki + i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot b + (k \sqrt{3} - ki) c. \end{align*}We want the coefficients of $a$, $b$, and $c$ to be equal on both sides. Equating the coefficients of $c$, we get \[1 - ki = k \sqrt{3} - ki,\]so $k = 1/\sqrt{3}$. For this value of $k$, both coefficients of $a$ become $-1 - i/\sqrt{3}$, and both coefficients of $b$ become $2i/\sqrt{3}$. Hence, the value of $k$ that works is $k = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Hence, \[a' = a + ki(c - b).\]Similarly, \begin{align*} b' &= b + ki(a - c), \\ c' &= c + ki(b - a). \end{align*}[asy] unitsize(0.6 cm); pair[] A, B, C; pair D, E, F; A[0] = (2,4); B[0] = (0,1); C[0] = (5,0); D = (A[0] + reflect(B[0],C[0])*(A[0]))/2; E = (B[0] + reflect(C[0],A[0])*(B[0]))/2; F = (C[0] + reflect(A[0],B[0])*(C[0]))/2; A[1] = A[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(C[0] - B[0])); B[1] = B[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(A[0] - C[0])); C[1] = C[0] + (1/sqrt(3))*(rotate(90)*(B[0] - A[0])); draw(A[0]--B[0]--C[0]--cycle); draw(A[1]--D); draw(B[1]--E); draw(C[1]--F); draw(B[1]--A[1]--C[1],dashed); label("$a$", A[0], NW); dot("$a'$", A[1], N); label("$b$", B[0], S); dot("$b'$", B[1], SW); label("$c$", C[0], S); dot("$c'$", C[1], SE); [/asy] We want triangle $A'B'C'$ to be equilateral, so we want $a'$, $b'$, and $c'$ to satisfy \[c' - a' = e^{\pi i/3} (b' - a').\]Substituting our expressions for $a'$, $b'$, and $c'$, and using the fact that \[e^{\pi i/3} = \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i,\]we get \[c + ki(b - a) - a - ki(c - b) = \left( \frac{1}{2} + \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} i \right) [b + ki(a - c) - a - ki(c - b)].\]Expanding and simplifying both sides, we get \begin{align*} &(-1 - ki) a + 2ki b + (1 - ki) c \\ &= \frac{-k \sqrt{3} - 1 + ki - i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot a + \frac{- k \sqrt{3} + 1 + ki + i \sqrt{3}}{2} \cdot b + (k \sqrt{3} - ki) c. \end{align*}We want the coefficients of $a$, $b$, and $c$ to be equal on both sides. Equating the coefficients of $c$, we get \[1 - ki = k \sqrt{3} - ki,\]so $k = 1/\sqrt{3}$. For this value of $k$, both coefficients of $a$ become $-1 - i/\sqrt{3}$, and both coefficients of $b$ become $2i/\sqrt{3}$. Hence, the value of $k$ that works is $k = \boxed{\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}}$.

Problem: Determine whether the following statement is true or false: If \((n^{2} a_{n})\) is a convergent sequence, then there exists a subsequence \((x_{n_k})\) of \(x_{n}=\frac{\sin (n)}{n}\) such that the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{n_k} a_{k}\) is convergent. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: True \boxed{True}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if this statement is true or false. Let me start by breaking it down. The statement says: If (n² aₙ) is a convergent sequence, then there exists a subsequence (x_{n_k}) of xₙ = sin(n)/n such that the series ∑_{k=1}^∞ x_{n_k} a_k converges. First, let's parse the given conditions and the conclusion. The hypothesis is that n² aₙ converges. So, that means as n approaches infinity, n² aₙ approaches some limit, say L. Therefore, aₙ must be behaving like L / n² for large n. So, aₙ is O(1/n²). Which would imply that the series ∑ aₙ converges absolutely, since the sum of 1/n² converges. Then, the conclusion is that there exists a subsequence of xₙ = sin(n)/n such that the series ∑ x_{n_k} a_k converges. So, xₙ itself is sin(n)/n, which is a bounded sequence because |sin(n)| ≤ 1, so |xₙ| ≤ 1/n. Then, the terms xₙ are O(1/n), and the series ∑ xₙ is conditionally convergent (since ∑ sin(n)/n converges conditionally by Dirichlet's test, but not absolutely because ∑ 1/n diverges). But here we're allowed to take a subsequence of xₙ, so x_{n_k} = sin(n_k)/n_k, and sum over k of sin(n_k)/n_k * a_k. The question is whether such a subsequence exists so that this series converges. Given that aₖ is O(1/k²), then each term x_{n_k} a_k is O(1/(n_k k²)). But since n_k is a subsequence, n_k ≥ k, right? Because in a subsequence, the indices are increasing. So n_k ≥ k for all k. Therefore, 1/(n_k k²) ≤ 1/k³. So then the terms x_{n_k} a_k are O(1/k³), and ∑ 1/k³ converges absolutely. Therefore, |x_{n_k} a_k| ≤ (1/n_k)(C /k²) ≤ C / (k^3). Therefore, the terms are bounded by C /k³, whose sum converges. Therefore, by the comparison test, the series ∑ |x_{n_k} a_k| converges, so the original series ∑ x_{n_k} a_k converges absolutely. By the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} |x_{n_k} a_k|\) converges absolutely. Hence, the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{n_k} a_k\) converges absolutely for any subsequence \((x_{n_k})\). Since any subsequence will work, there certainly exists such a subsequence. Therefore, the statement is true. \[ \boxed{True} \]

Therefore, the statement is true. \[ \boxed{True} \]

By the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} |x_{n_k} a_k|\) converges absolutely. Hence, the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{n_k} a_k\) converges absolutely for any subsequence \((x_{n_k})\). Since any subsequence will work, there certainly exists such a subsequence. Therefore, the statement is true. \[ \boxed{True} \]

Therefore, regardless of the signs, the series ∑ x_{n_k} aₖ converges absolutely. This implies that the series \(\sum a_n\) converges absolutely. We need to determine if there exists a subsequence \((x_{n_k})\) of \(x_n = \frac{\sin(n)}{n}\) such that the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{n_k} a_k\) converges. Therefore, the product term \(|x_{n_k} a_k| \leq \frac{C}{n_k k^2} \leq \frac{C}{k^3}\). The series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{k^3}\) is a convergent p-series with \(p = 3 > 1\). By the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} |x_{n_k} a_k|\) converges absolutely. Hence, the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{n_k} a_k\) converges absolutely for any subsequence \((x_{n_k})\). Since any subsequence will work, there certainly exists such a subsequence. Therefore, the statement is true. \[ \boxed{True} \]

The statement says: If (n² aₙ) is a convergent sequence, then there exists a subsequence (x_{n_k}) of xₙ = sin(n)/n such that the series ∑_{k=1}^∞ x_{n_k} a_k converges. First, let's parse the given conditions and the conclusion. The hypothesis is that n² aₙ converges. So, that means as n approaches infinity, n² aₙ approaches some limit, say L. Therefore, aₙ must be behaving like L / n² for large n. So, aₙ is O(1/n²). Which would imply that the series ∑ aₙ converges absolutely, since the sum of 1/n² converges. Then, the conclusion is that there exists a subsequence of xₙ = sin(n)/n such that the series ∑ x_{n_k} a_k converges. So, xₙ itself is sin(n)/n, which is a bounded sequence because |sin(n)| ≤ 1, so |xₙ| ≤ 1/n. Then, the terms xₙ are O(1/n), and the series ∑ xₙ is conditionally convergent (since ∑ sin(n)/n converges conditionally by Dirichlet's test, but not absolutely because ∑ 1/n diverges). But here we're allowed to take a subsequence of xₙ, so x_{n_k} = sin(n_k)/n_k, and sum over k of sin(n_k)/n_k * a_k. The question is whether such a subsequence exists so that this series converges. Given that aₖ is O(1/k²), then each term x_{n_k} a_k is O(1/(n_k k²)). But since n_k is a subsequence, n_k ≥ k, right? Because in a subsequence, the indices are increasing. So n_k ≥ k for all k. Therefore, 1/(n_k k²) ≤ 1/k³. So then the terms x_{n_k} a_k are O(1/k³), and ∑ 1/k³ converges absolutely. Given that \(n_k \geq k\) for all \(k\), it follows that \(\frac{1}{n_k} \leq \frac{1}{k}\). Given \(a_k\) is \(O(1/k^2)\), we have \(|a_k| \leq \frac{C}{k^2}\) for some constant \(C\). Therefore, the product term \(|x_{n_k} a_k| \leq \frac{C}{n_k k^2} \leq \frac{C}{k^3}\). The series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{k^3}\) is a convergent p-series with \(p = 3 > 1\). By the comparison test, the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} |x_{n_k} a_k|\) converges absolutely. Hence, the series \(\sum_{k=1}^{\infty} x_{n_k} a_k\) converges absolutely for any subsequence \((x_{n_k})\). Since any subsequence will work, there certainly exists such a subsequence. Therefore, the statement is true. \[ \boxed{True} \]

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✓, Reward: 1.00

Therefore, 1/(n_k k²) ≤ 1/k³. So then the terms x_{n_k} a_k are O(1/k³), and ∑ 1/k³ converges absolutely. do_single_rollout

Problem: Find all solutions to the equation \[\frac{\left(\frac{x}{x+1}\right)^2 + 11}{\left(\frac{x}{x+1}\right)^2 + 1} = 2.\]Enter all the solutions, separated by commas. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 1 \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: We make the substitution $y = \left(\frac{x}{x+1}\right)^2$ to simplify the equation, so that \[\frac{y+11}{y+1} = 2.\]Multiplying by $y+1$ gives $y+11 = 2y+2,$ so $y=9.$ Therefore, we have \[\frac{x}{x+1} = \pm 3.\]Then, either $x = 3(x+1)$ or $x = -3(x+1).$ These give solutions $x =\boxed{ -\tfrac32}$ and $x = \boxed{-\tfrac34},$ respectively.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00
